What does that mean and what are the implications of that?DO:Okay. So, first and foremost, I think this is a relatively stale take. If you know, you know, you‚Äôve known for a while, but pretty much they‚Äôre shifting-Right, you made this call a long time ago, to be clear, it‚Äôs sort of coming true now, but I think a lot of people are not as deep into it as you, so I‚Äôm going to pretend it‚Äôs fresh.DO:EUV and the EUV revolutionwas this ginormous, huge platform shift within semiconductors, maybe not a platform shift, but you understand, a huge technological inflection. I think there‚Äôs maybe tens of steps using EUV layers, but it‚Äôs thousands of steps to make a chip, and so what was pretty clear is if you go to the IEEE conferences, that the future of technological innovation for semiconductors is not going to be EUV, the shrinking pitch is not where the future is going, and that means focusing on where the next inflections are.The shift from planarto FinFETwas this first big, big change and then, from FinFETto gate-all-aroundis the next technological inflection. So that is going to come almost exclusively like isotropic etch,ALD, materials specifically. Everyone is telling you at all these conferences, ‚ÄúOh my God, this is going to be a much more important part of the process, this is the next bottleneck‚Äù, and then, I think most people are looking at the last bottleneck and saying, ‚ÄúOh, this bottleneck happens forever‚Äù. If they just take more and more and more share, your chip would cost too much to make, it‚Äôs all about shifting bottlenecks. If all the customers are focused on lowering EUV layers and doing different technologies like ALD or isotropic etch or different materials, then EUV will shrink as a portion. And I‚Äôm not talking back to the pre-EUV like DUV levels‚ÄîIt‚Äôll stabilize.DO:Yeah, it‚Äôll stabilize. I think it will go down from the absolute peak, but we‚Äôre talking a few percentage here. And then the other aspect is that it‚Äôs very important to see scaling of things beyond just logic.DO:Yep.DP:Everyone‚Äôs focused on logic, and we‚Äôve gone through a period where logic grew very fast and memory did not. Memory consolidated down to three players ‚Äî they kept cutting and cutting, at least DRAM, so you kind of had the percentage of spend going to logic really skyrocket. Now you kind of see this shifting back to memory, especially with AI. Obviously, logic is still huge and growing and Nvidia and others want to get to 3nm and 2nm as fast as possible, but HBM [High Bandwidth Memory] is really where a lot of this spend shift is going. And with memory in general, memory hit the bottleneck of Moore‚Äôs Law a decade before logic did.Yeah.DP:Memory stopped scaling in 2008 to 2012 in terms of cost scaling, of like humongous cost scaling improvements every two years, and it dropped down to like 10 to 15% improvements every year, so that was a humongous slowdown that logic didn‚Äôt hit until the latter part of last decade, or really early part of this decade, so that also shifted. People kept scaling on logic, especially EUV lithography, and lithography was huge there, but now when you look at DRAM, lithography is also tapping out. It‚Äôs already tapped out a while ago, and people have been working on, ‚ÄúHey, how do we improve past it?‚Äù, and it turns out, everything goes 3D. Price is coming down a bit, but it‚Äôs more a harder wall to break through. Basically, all of these tool vendors in China are behind. If you were to build a process technology with only Chinese vendors, it‚Äôs quite a bit behind, but much like a jigsaw puzzle, if I take one piece away from you, you‚Äôll know I took one piece away from you and you‚Äôll be able to figure it out.In that sense, this is exactly what China does, is when they build a fab, they will buy a bunch of the foreign tools, and then they‚Äôll also build a bunch of their own tools, or buy it from their domestic companies like Anora or AMAC or what have you, and then they‚Äôll stick those tools right next to each other and route wafers that are at the same point in manufacturing to different tools. Then they‚Äôll say, ‚ÄúHey, what‚Äôs the data here? What‚Äôs the data there, we‚Äôre going to replicate it. Oh, okay, here‚Äôs how we tune our tool, here‚Äôs how we improve the tool. Okay, we‚Äôll send the new generation of it that works better. Oh, it‚Äôs still off in these ways. Let‚Äôs improve it again‚Äù. So they iterate, iterate, iterate, while having just one piece of the jigsaw puzzle. So it‚Äôs like, ‚ÄúOh wait, Intel‚Äôs actually fine on a client, and that‚Äôs what Qualcomm is good at, so there‚Äôs no synergies here‚Äù.Yeah, I think that sounds right.AWS and the HyperscalersWell, speaking of the hyperscalers and the data center, Dylan, you wrote an article a couple of years ago about howAmazon was losing in AI. Can you re-summarize your argument? And has your view changed because Amazon has changed or what‚Äôs the sort of current status now?DP:So Amazon was losing in AI because their entire cloud was hyper-focused on a general purpose workload, and they crush it. They are still much better on a cost basis, cost structure than Google or Microsoft Azure or Oracle with their Nitro NICs, with their custom transport that they‚Äôve built, Elastic Fabric Adapter, with their Graviton CPUs, and the list goes on and on. This is part of the reason why they‚Äôre the best hyperscaler at silicon is what they‚Äôve commonly been thought of as. But that only exists out on general purpose workloads.That‚Äôs right. They‚Äôre a bullet train and it‚Äôs hard to turn a bullet train.DP:Yeah, exactly. So now when you look at, with AI workloads, we‚Äôll have a piece coming out on this soon, but we‚Äôve rented 256 GPUs at, this is like a hundred grand by the way, at Google, at Amazon, at NeoClouds who have graciously just let us use them because they have them available, but whatever and we‚Äôve tested 256 GPUs on Nvidia‚Äôs version of Ethernet, Nvidia‚Äôs InfiniBand, on Google‚Äôs version of Ethernet, on Amazon‚Äôs version of Ethernet and it turns out it sucks,Elastic Fabric Adapteris much worse, even at the same theoretical maximums.It‚Äôs not made for this job.DP:Yeah, it‚Äôs not made for this job and so they‚Äôve had a real big problem there. Now when you look at like, ‚ÄúWell, what is the flip side?‚Äù ‚Äî you also have an organization, a massive, massive organization who has shown tangible improvements by messing with their suppliers, not messing with them, but modifying it, customizing, adding their own stuff, bringing their philosophy into it. Massive organization, tens of thousands of people, and now you tell them, ‚ÄúHey, you‚Äôre all wrong, just buy what Nvidia sells you and shut up‚Äù. It‚Äôs like, this is not going to happen. Politically, this just does not work. Microsoft, to their credit, is like, ‚ÄúYes, I don‚Äôt care, I‚Äôll use InfiniBand. I don‚Äôt care, I‚Äôll use whatever you tell me, Nvidia‚Äù.This is the Microsoft story. Basically, once they let go of the Windows mindset, they‚Äôre like, ‚ÄúLinux, okay, Linux it is, we‚Äôre going to sell box standard Linux servers, here you go‚Äù. They were forced into pragmatism in a certain respect, which then served them well once you got to the AI wave.DP:Amazon sort of has that issue, in that they‚Äôre behind on AI, it‚Äôs hard to turn the bullet train as you said, and then on top of that, their infrastructure is not really well optimized to it. Now they are turning the ship. I think they‚Äôve made some key hires as well as key movement around in the company to get the AI silicon teams more into gear and in that sense, I‚Äôm actually more hopeful forTrainium from Amazonthan I am fromMaia from Microsoftor from ‚Äî not quite TPU level, but I‚Äôm more hopeful of Trainium long-termthan Inferentia.But there is a significant challenge here in that like, ‚ÄúHey, when is Amazon going to have their first GB200s deployed on the cloud?‚Äù. Four to six months after Microsoft. ‚ÄúWhen are they going to have their first models trained on GB200?‚Äù, probably a significant time difference. And then at the same time they have this, ‚ÄúHey, we‚Äôre going toinvest hugely in Anthropicand try and make them similar to our champions, similar to how OpenAI and Microsoft work together‚Äù, which is great, they need that because before they didn‚Äôt have any champion of AI model, so they were so far behind on AI models. The open source models are better than any model that Amazon‚Äôs ever trained. So you sort of have this very big challenge that hey, maybe this deal with Anthropic also comes with, ‚ÄúAnthropic, you must use a lot of our own chips‚Äù, in which case that could hold Anthropic back. But at the same time, Anthropic is like, ‚ÄúWell, I need to build a $10 billion cluster to compete‚Äù.And they don‚Äôt have hundreds of billions of revenue to begin with.DO:They‚Äôll do it for $10 billion. You‚Äôre like, ‚ÄúOh, maybe I‚Äôll change my mind at the tune of $25 billion in Trainium credits‚Äù, I think pretty much Amazon got here by bullying their suppliers, by creating a bullet train, by pushing costs and especially on the CPU side.DP:And thinking outside the box too.DO:Yeah, especially, I mean they‚Äôre really creative on the data center side especially.The whole Nitro architecture is brilliant for CPU workloads.DO:Yeah, and here‚Äôs the thing is what got them here was this culture and mindset. Asking them to not be who they are is like it‚Äôs not going to work, and so Trainium is going to be the true manifestation of Amazon‚Äôs philosophy of data centers, I think that‚Äôs the difference. So you have the Amazon philosophy where it‚Äôs just, ‚ÄúMake my boxes in bigger volume‚Äù, essentially, it‚Äôs like a private label. It‚Äôs a Amazon Basics version of a data center. Maybe it‚Äôs 20% cheaper or maybe it‚Äôs like 20% worse, but it‚Äôs 40% cheaper and we‚Äôll beat them through scale and boom, all of a sudden eventually you can‚Äôt compete against us because we have so much scale. That‚Äôs what got them here versus the Nvidia approach of, ‚ÄúWe will be the best performing product at all costs‚Äù, and so I think that there‚Äôs two different philosophies here.Amazon‚Äôs philosophy was really outdated, but I think they‚Äôre going to stick with it and Trainium will be interesting with enough resources will they be able to brute force it? Maybe, and I think Anthropic really is the perfect partner for it. CUDA is definitely a moat, but with enough incentives with the right people, you can definitely train a frontier model on Trainium. TPU‚Äôs shown that you can do it and Amazon is much more aggressive and I think that we‚Äôre about to see, I‚Äôm cautiously optimistic for Trainium as well. Definitely more so than Maia and it‚Äôs hilarious because Google, we haven‚Äôt even talked about them in this entire time, because ‚ÄúHey, the single best custom silicon training thing and they‚Äôre super conservative‚Äù, it‚Äôs just kind of weird. They‚Äôve been gifted a software and hardware platform from God already because they already made it five years ago and they‚Äôre not doubling down on their lead.Is there a bit where Amazon and Google go their own way? Maybe it turns out that Amazon, just because of the networking architecture and whatnot, they never are as good at training, but for relatively small scale inference, which may end up being the vast majority of tasks, it‚Äôs fine and they have a sort of cost advantage. Google is like the God of training, they have this massively scalable cross-data center, you mentioned that capability before and then Meta and Microsoft are kind of AMD shops maybe for inference to the extent they can. And of course all this lording over it is Nvidia just the pure best performance, the biggest developer ecosystem, all the sorts of advantages that go there. Using their API up until a week ago was annoying.It‚Äôs because Google is an integrated player. They‚Äôre the Apple of the cloud and Apple, it‚Äôs hard to play with others too, it‚Äôs actually, I think it‚Äôs a cultural thing.DP:Yeah, so they‚Äôve just had so many issues here that are confusing to me/decisions that are confusing. One that Doug was alluding to earlier is like, ‚ÄúHey, Google has the best silicon has the biggest training clusters today, where‚Äôs their model that crushes GPT-4?‚Äù. Or, ‚ÄúHey, they have so much better silicon, why are they not building data centers at the same pace that Microsoft is the same pace that Meta is?‚Äù. They‚Äôre just not being nearly as aggressive as Amazon even at building data centers, what are these decisions that they‚Äôre making? Why are they, of all of the companies having the most financial sort of like-Restraint?DP:Let‚Äôs say, yeah, restraint.DO:It‚Äôs like having pocket aces and you‚Äôre like, ‚ÄúI‚Äôm folding, guys, I‚Äôm folding‚Äù. They have this giant opportunity and maybe this is a [Google CEO] Sundar [Pichai] thing. He doesn‚Äôt like CEO, he‚Äôs a renter, he doesn‚Äôt have the political capital or something like that. I mean, I definitely heard that theory kind of pushed around, but I don‚Äôt know what it is because it‚Äôs really odd. In this competition of the hyperscalers pushing for the big frontier models, Google has an amazing hand and they just don‚Äôt play it aggressively. And why? Who knows?Nvidia and PassportDid [Nvidia CEO] Jensen [Huang] push it too far with this whole sell Nvidia chips to NeoClouds to sort of hedge against the hyperscalers strategy, and leading tomesses like Super Microor whatever the heck‚Äôs going on there?DO:I don‚Äôt know. You want to throw some crackpot theories? I know we have quite a few.DP:I mean, I think strategically it‚Äôs what he has to do, and this is part of why Amazon is kind of like ‚Äî Amazon won a lot because they had the best database software, it was very easy to use and set up. In a world where the cloud is not providing the software for running AI, a lot of Amazon‚Äôs advantages go away. Being easy to use, like, ‚ÄúOh, okay, great, I‚Äôll just download VLM or Tensor RTL and I‚Äôll download this open source model and it‚Äôll run‚Äù, and it‚Äôs not that hard. So there‚Äôs 40 of them out there, this has been a big boon for us, we‚Äôve helped people with this as well. There‚Äôs so many things out there that a small team of people can set up a cloud that‚Äôs decent and so then the question is, did Jensen go too far? The effort on top of that to just do the software for it is so smallest percentage of the effort.And the amount of data ingress-egress is so low relatively speaking, it‚Äôs fine.DO:Yeah. So it‚Äôs like what‚Äôs the point? If someone else is willing to do it for a cheaper margin and you‚Äôre like, ‚ÄúOkay, we can own this full stack, or we could have a faster go-to-market and we can bully our supplier down the road and we can force them to do SLAs, we don‚Äôt have to hire all this‚Äù. O‚ÄôLaughlin is the author ofFabricated Knowledge, a Substack that covers the semiconductor industry, primarily from a financial perspective. Patel is the author and founder ofSemiAnalysis, a newsletter, consulting, and research firm focused on the semiconductor supply chain.I previously interviewed O‚ÄôLaughlin and Patelin September 2023, and since then SemiAnalysis has grown to be the authoritative research firm for the AI supply chain in particular. That growth includes O‚ÄôLaughlin: while Fabricated Knowledge will continue to be a standalone publication, O‚ÄôLaughlin has teamed up with Patel to drive the expanding SemiAnalysis team to new heights. One ingredient in that growth,as I noted on Monday, is Passport; SemiAnalysis is the first 3rd-party entity to use the software I have developed to power Stratechery.In this interview we discuss Patel and O‚ÄôLaughlin‚Äôs vision for SemiAnalysis, and whey they think AI represents an opportunity to build a leading research firm. We then move onto all things chips, including the semiconductor supply chain, China, Intel, hyperscalers generally and AWS specifically, and why O‚ÄôLaughlin, one of the longest and loudest Nvidia bulls, has some long-term concerns.As a reminder, all Stratechery content, including interviews, is available as a podcast; click the link at the top of this email to add Stratechery to your podcast player.On to the Interview:An Interview with Dylan Patel and Doug O‚ÄôLaughlin About the Current State of Semiconductors and SemiAnalysisThis interview is lightly edited for clarity.Topics:The SemiAnalysis Vision|The Semi Supply Chain|China|Intel|AWS and the Hyperscalers|Nvidia and PassportThe SemiAnalysis VisionDoug O‚ÄôLaughlin and Dylan Patel, welcome back to Stratechery.Doug O‚ÄôLauhglin:Nice to be here.DP:Thank you for having us.The last time you were here, actually we were in person, you were here in Taipei, we were stuck in an incredibly hot room with a broken air conditioner, it was a nice bonding experience. If you were to build a process technology with only Chinese vendors, it‚Äôs quite a bit behind, but much like a jigsaw puzzle, if I take one piece away from you, you‚Äôll know I took one piece away from you and you‚Äôll be able to figure it out.In that sense, this is exactly what China does, is when they build a fab, they will buy a bunch of the foreign tools, and then they‚Äôll also build a bunch of their own tools, or buy it from their domestic companies like Anora or AMAC or what have you, and then they‚Äôll stick those tools right next to each other and route wafers that are at the same point in manufacturing to different tools. I did want to have one question though, Doug, when you wrote about the ASML a couple of weeks ago, you made the point that just the reality of the semi supply chain is you basically have almost like a chain of monopolies, especially when you get to the supplier layer, but they‚Äôre still limited by the overall market size in growth. The change for ASML is not that they‚Äôre becoming less important, maybe a little bit, but that there‚Äôs one buyer and so they‚Äôre going to grow with the market, which is still a very healthy place to be.The question is, is there any concern you have about, broadly speaking, brittleness in this world? I remember having a thesis on a share gain for process control, and essentially the answer is no one actually ever takes share in the long run, they all just trade share. There‚Äôs a few inflections where it does happen, but more often than not, the share gains ‚Äî everyone says they‚Äôre gaining share from each other. You actually look at the board and we‚Äôre talking like bps against each other, EUV was truly a little bit of a very special story.So I think that the reality is the monopolies are going to continue to be entrenched, but the part where I think the brittleness comes from is maybe the under-appreciated story for the next ten years is China. I think theFab Whack-A-Mole piece, and I love that I can pump SemiAnalysis pieces now because I obviously read the hell out of them and I talk to and work with people who write them and help edit and stuff, then do all kinds of stuff.But the Fab Whack-A-Mole piece really puts it well, because if you look on that spectrum, there‚Äôs a graph that shows the spectrum of easy-to-be-replicated by China, hard-to-be-replicated by China. You use the Western thing as much as possible, but you also develop your own industry, and they do it in a really cool, somewhat decentralized way. Basically, all of these tool vendors in China are behind. If you were to build a process technology with only Chinese vendors, it‚Äôs quite a bit behind, but much like a jigsaw puzzle, if I take one piece away from you, you‚Äôll know I took one piece away from you and you‚Äôll be able to figure it out.In that sense, this is exactly what China does, is when they build a fab, they will buy a bunch of the foreign tools, and then they‚Äôll also build a bunch of their own tools, or buy it from their domestic companies like Anora or AMAC or what have you, and then they‚Äôll stick those tools right next to each other and route wafers that are at the same point in manufacturing to different tools. Then they‚Äôll say, ‚ÄúHey, what‚Äôs the data here? What‚Äôs the data there, we‚Äôre going to replicate it. Oh, okay, here‚Äôs how we tune our tool, here‚Äôs how we improve the tool. Okay, we‚Äôll send the new generation of it that works better. Oh, it‚Äôs still off in these ways. Let‚Äôs improve it again‚Äù. So they iterate, iterate, iterate, while having just one piece of the jigsaw puzzle. Now you do that across every single tool and every single step, and then it ends up being, for many of these tools, because we have the state-of-the-art sitting literally right next to ‚Äî we‚Äôve taken apart some of the state of the art ‚Äî we can catch up in a few years. Then for some of these other tools, it is just really that complicated and it‚Äôs going to take five, ten years, probably closer to that five number honestly. DP:I think China fully understands that you do both. Basically, all of these tool vendors in China are behind. If you were to build a process technology with only Chinese vendors, it‚Äôs quite a bit behind, but much like a jigsaw puzzle, if I take one piece away from you, you‚Äôll know I took one piece away from you and you‚Äôll be able to figure it out. In that sense, this is exactly what China does, is when they build a fab, they will buy a bunch of the foreign tools, and then they‚Äôll also build a bunch of their own tools, or buy it from their domestic companies like Anora or AMAC or what have you, and then they‚Äôll stick those tools right next to each other and route wafers that are at the same point in manufacturing to different tools. Then they‚Äôll say, ‚ÄúHey, what‚Äôs the data here? What‚Äôs the data there, we‚Äôre going to replicate it. Oh, okay, here‚Äôs how we tune our tool, here‚Äôs how we improve the tool. Okay, we‚Äôll send the new generation of it that works better. Oh, it‚Äôs still off in these ways. Let‚Äôs improve it again‚Äù. So they iterate, iterate, iterate, while having just one piece of the jigsaw puzzle. In that context, the risk of going with a new process and it not working out, you‚Äôre not endangering your logic spend, you‚Äôre endangering the entire product revenue, and so you have this real mismatch of risk of it going wrong versus potential savings or relatively small gains as part of an overall system. We in the United States of America can only build so much power because of all sorts of reasons, industrial policy, industrial capacity, permitting, regulatory, all this sort of stuff and therefore a chip, while maybe it costs the same, maybe it costs twice as much, but if I only have a gigawatt to build the biggest freaking AI cluster I can in this one spot, I better do it, I better get the highest performance chip and build it with that chip.
